<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="1ca14655e96a4126b7084f11c518be7f"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1ca14655e96a4126b7084f11c518be7f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '176fb572-431a-4b2e-bdae-b2fab3cc3935'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d83639740" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The development of highly efficient sodium-ion batteries depends critically on the successful exploitation of advanced anode hosts that is capable of overcoming sluggish reaction kinetics while also withstanding severe structural deformation triggered by the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion. Herein, a hierarchically hybrid material with hetero-Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS hollow nanosphere packaged into a densified N-doped carbon matrix (Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C) was designed and fabricated utilizing CoNi-glycerate as the self-sacrifice template, making the utmost of the synergistic effect of hetero-Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS with strong electric field and rich reaction active-sites together with the densified outer-carbon scaffolds with remarkable electronic conductivity and robust mechanical toughness. As anticipated, as-fabricated Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C anode affords remarkable specific capacity, prolonged cycle lifespan up to 2 400 cycles with an only 0.05% fading each cycle at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and excellent rate feature (354.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>), one of the best performances for most existing Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS-based anodes. Ex situ structural characterizations in tandem with theoretical analysis demonstrate the reversible insertion-conversion mechanism of initially proceeding with Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation and superior heterogeneous interfacial reaction behavior with strong Na<sup>+</sup>-adsorption ability. Further, sodium-ion full cell and hybrid capacitor based on Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C anode exhibit impressive electrochemical characteristics on cycling performance and rate capability, showcasing its outstanding feasibility toward practical use.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sus2198-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sus2198-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Globally, the use of renewable energy sources has been increasing sharply in recent years, which drive the development of new-fashioned battery technologies and chemistries to enable their high-efficiency use into practice.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0001_R_d83639732e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - SusMat">1</a>-<a href="#sus2198-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0003_R_d83639732e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv Funct Mater">3</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have motivated intense interest with a view to the merits of sodium with low cost, sustainability of Na supplies, and chemical similarity to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0004_R_d83639732e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - SusMat">4</a>-<a href="#sus2198-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0010_R_d83639732e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochem Energy Rev">10</a></sup></span> The widespread commercial use of SIBs is however faced with the significant challenges, involving poor electrochemical activity, relatively great ion-diffusion barrier, and gravimetric energy density of anode materials, mainly thanks to larger ion-radius and heavier ionic-weigh of Na<sup>+</sup> in comparison to Li<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0011_R_d83639732e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - SusMat">11</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0012_R_d83639732e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J Mater Chem A">12</a></sup></span> Under these circumstances, frontier efforts toward exploiting SIBs anodes have primarily highlighted on the efficient use of double-type reaction-based anode matter (e.g., insertion-conversion or conversion-alloy), enabling SIBs to match existing LIBs in energy densities.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0013_R_d83639732e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - SusMat">13</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0014_R_d83639732e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - SusMat">14</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>As desirable double-type ion-storage anode, transition metal chalcogenides (TMCs), renowned for their more significant electronic conductivity, better redox reversibility, and smaller volumetric variations than their oxide counterparts, have been envisaged as the attractive matter for high-efficiency energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0015_R_d83639732e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv Funct Mater">15</a>-<a href="#sus2198-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0017_R_d83639732e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J Energy Chem">17</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the final conversion product Na<sub>2</sub>S formed by TMCs during the charge–discharge reaction is more conductive than discharge product Na<sub>2</sub>O of oxide counterparts, kinetically facilitating better ion-diffusion and electrochemical activity.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0018_R_d83639732e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0019_R_d83639732e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem Eng J">19</a></sup></span> Not only that TMCs have a higher specific capacity and better safety in comparison to anode matter based on single intercalation-type Na<sup>+</sup>-storage materials (such as carbon anode) since they prevent the formation of sodium dendrite in the low voltage area.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0020_R_d83639732e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span> Of the reported TMCs represented by Ni/Co-based anodes, famous for the intercalation–conversion mechanism, have distinguished themselves as desired contenders for SIBs by virtue of their outstanding theoretical capacity, strong redox activity, easy fabrication, and cost-effectiveness.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0021_R_d83639732e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J Ind Eng Chem">21</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0022_R_d83639732e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J Colloid Interf Sci">22</a></sup></span> And a step further is the Ni/Co-based TMCs with diverse structural forms and more elemental valence states that can endow more abundant Na<sup>+</sup>-storage sites. Unfortunately, three main obstacles associated with inferior kinetics brought by inherent poor conductivity, restricted cyclability on account of the significant volume fluctuation and new phases formation, together with the polysulfides dissolution in electrolyte during the repeated Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion still heavily restricted their practical application.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0023_R_d83639732e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew Chem Int Ed">23</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0024_R_d83639732e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Nano">24</a></sup></span> The ion conductivity and swift ion-transport velocity are generally very important factors that determine a material's ability to store energy.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0025_R_d83639732e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem Eng J">25</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0026_R_d83639732e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ Sci">26</a></sup></span> Hence, it is of great significance and urgency to synthesize and build a unique Ni/Co-based TMCs anode with better electronic/ionic transfer rate, aiming to provide exceptional durability and sufficient rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>As reported, the decoration of the carbon matrix with active electrode materials is feasible for substantially increasing reaction kinetics, alleviating the unavoidable pulverization brought by the drastic structural distortion, and stabilizing solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI).<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0027_R_d83639732e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem Eng J">27</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0028_R_d83639732e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span> More specifically, heteroatom-doped carbon matrix can increase active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>-storage, change the electron feature of the carbon matrix, and increase the interlayer distance, making it easier for electron to conduct in active materials.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0029_R_d83639732e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - InfoMat">29</a></sup></span> Although carbon-based TMCs hybrid structures perform better electrochemically, while the conventional carbon modification above was concentrated on the external interface regulation or conductive substrate to stabilize the electrode structure and facilitate interparticle electronic/ionic diffusion on the electrode surface, which cannot effectively expedite rapid ion-transport between the crystal structure, thereby restricting further enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup>-storage ability for carbon-based TMCs hybrids.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0030_R_d83639732e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv Funct Mater">30</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0031_R_d83639732e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Storage Mater">31</a></sup></span> Recently reported demonstrates that constructing multicomponent sulfides utilizing two kinds of diverse electronic band-gap components are regarded as one of the most efficient intrinsic crystal–interface models to promote the internal charge-transfer and ion-transport.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0032_R_d83639732e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv Funct Mater">32</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0033_R_d83639732e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span> Indeed, the existence of heterointerface helps to stabilize the structural stability and avoid the agglomeration of metal nanoparticles produced by the conversion reaction, which improved reversibility and species absorptivity. Meanwhile, a localized built-in electric field is spontaneously generated on the interface by such a heterogeneous boundary, which significantly enhance electronic conductivity and charge carrier mobility, resulting in a rapid ion shuttle rate and strong energy storage capabilities.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0034_R_d83639732e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv Funct Mater">34</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0035_R_d83639732e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Mater Chem A">35</a></sup></span> What's more impressive is that the heterogeneous structure not only contains rich phase interfaces that can contribute to excess capacity by creating specific capacitance effects and additional ion storage sites,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0036_R_d83639732e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat Energy">36</a></sup></span> but also the out-of-step redox voltages and electrochemical reactions of multicomponent can afford significant electrochemical activity and decrease volumetric strain as a result of the complementary and synergistic interaction between the reaction products.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0037_R_d83639732e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv Mater">37</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0038_R_d83639732e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> It is therefore very desirable, but still faces tremendous challenge, for exploiting multicomponent composites with heterojunction and hierarchical carbon decoration to resolve major bottlenecks upon the cycling through the collaborative mechanism between the components.</p>
         
         <p>Enlighted from above analysis, we put forward a straightforward method for designing a hierarchically hybrid material with hetero-Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS hollow nanosphere encapsulated into an ultrathin N-doped carbon matrix (Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C), which establishes a compacted spatial geometry configuration via a combination strategy of vulcanization, in situ dopamine polymerization, and carbonization involving CoNi-glycerate as the template. This specially hierarchical hybrid structure has the significant ability that can induce swift charge-transfer and ion-migration, afford abundant Na<sup>+</sup>-transport channels, as well as supply an adequate number of active sites and electrode/electrolyte contact interface. Additionally, the volumetric strain of the electrode can also be simultaneously cushioned by the strong coupling interaction of the multiphase constituent particularly as cycled at high rate. Results from the theoretical analysis and ex situ characterization further expound the electrochemical reaction mechanism and the interplay between carbon decoration and heterostructure. Consequently, as-fabricated Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C anode manifests remarkable Na<sup>+</sup>-storage characteristics from the perspective of rate capability, reversible capacity, and cyclic lifespan.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sus2198-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sus2198-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <p>As manifested schematically in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1A</a>, a multi-step construction strategy was implemented to controllably fabricate the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites. Specifically, the CoNi-glycerate solid nanospheres (CoNi-G, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1B,C</a>), Co-glycerate solid nanospheres (Co-G, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), and Ni-glycerate solid nanospheres (Ni-G, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>) were selected as the precursor and synthesized via a facial hydrothermal method, which all present the uniform geometric nanospherical structure and monodisperse characteristic with smooth surface as confirmed by the field-emission scanning electrode microscopy (FESEM). Taking CoNi-G as an example, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1D,E</a> demonstrate its solid nature with a uniform diameter particle size of ∼500&nbsp;nm. Subsequently, CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> products were produced via hydrothermally induced chemical etching/anion exchange between CoNi-G and thioacetamide solution. As seen, the CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> sample well maintains the nanosphere shape of CoNi-G precursor (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b)</a>, presenting its superior structure stability. Meanwhile, the relatively rough surface and significant cavity region are visible in the TEM image of CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3c</a>). Besides, each CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanosphere has an average particle size of ∼600&nbsp;nm in diameter and a cavity wall of ∼20&nbsp;nm in thickness. According to the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d</a>), three types of interplanar spacings, involving (4 0 0), (1 1 1), and (2 2 0) facets, exist in the CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanospheres, demonstrating the successful synthesis of cubic CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, which can be further verified through X-ray diffraction (XRD) test (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). With the completion of in situ dopamine polymerization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>) and gas-phase carbonization procedure, an ultrathin and densified N-doped conductive carbon matrix was created by pyrolyzing dopamine (PDA), giving rise to the end-product Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C heterostructure. As revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1F,G</a>, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites almost maintain the same spherical architecture characteristic as hollow CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanosphere without any structural agglomeration and damage, and meanwhile the average particle size of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C is nearly equal to that of CoNi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@PDA. Further examination by TEM shows several regular hollow Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS sphere structure, which can be further wrapped through a uniform dense carbon layer of ∼20&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1H–J</a>). As the control sample, the FESEM and TEM images of hollow Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS nanospheres in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>&nbsp;show that the surface of nanospheres is rougher and there are obvious structural damage due to the absence of the outer-carbon matrix. A distinct phase boundary can be seen by comparing two different types of interplanar spacings, according to the HRTEM picture (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1K</a>). Two sets of distinct lattice fringes, which are 0.267 and 0.550&nbsp;nm, respectively, are readily good coincidence in the (1 0 1) plane of NiS and the (1 1 1) plane of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, indicating the co-presence of NiS and Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern also further signifies the presence of the polycrystalline nature for binary sulfides (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1L</a>), matching well with the (1 0 1) and (1 0 2) planes of NiS together with the (2 2 0), (3 1 1), and (4 0 0) planes of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. An additional indication of the co-existence of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS in the heterogeneous Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites is provided by Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0001">1M</a>, where the high angle annular dark field (HAADF) TEM imaging and associated energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) elemental mapping analysis clearly reveal that the distribution outlines of Co, Ni, S, C, and N elements continue to be the envisioned geometric microstructure of each nanosphere. As comparison, bare Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS hollow nanospheres can be obtained through directly vulcanizing Co-G and Ni-G nanospheres through hydrothermally induced chemical etching. One can see that the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> sample keeps almost the same spherical structure as Co-G nanospheres but has a rougher surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a>). Through TEM observation, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> sample exhibits a significant yolk–shell structure configuration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a>). For bare NiS sample, although the geometric hollow spherical feature is monitored, while the spherical surface is composed of many nanoparticles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sus2198-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13cb2ac1-a25b-47c2-9ac1-710b5348163b/sus2198-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13cb2ac1-a25b-47c2-9ac1-710b5348163b/sus2198-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/637abc8b-21c0-4707-a8eb-2812ebf2303b/sus2198-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13cb2ac1-a25b-47c2-9ac1-710b5348163b/sus2198-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">FIGURE 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sus2198-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsus2.198" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Detailed diagram of the synthetic routes of heterogeneous Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites. FESEM and TEM images at different magnifications for the samples: (B–E) CoNi-glycerate solid nanospheres, (F–J) hollow Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C nanospheres. (K) HRTEM graph with the matching line scan intensity profiles, (L) SAED pattern, and (M) EDX elemental mappings of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites. FESEM, Field-emission scanning electrode microscopy; TEM, transmission electron microscopy; SAED, selected area electron diffraction.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The XRD test was used to determine the substance composition and phase purity of as-constructed samples. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2A</a>, all the reflections appeared in bare Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS samples are in good accordance with the standard patterns of cubic Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS # 42-1448) and hexagonal NiS (JCPDS # 02-1280). Whereas for the heterogeneous two samples, the (2 2 0), (3 1 1), (4 0 0), (5 1 1), and (4 4 0) planes of cubic Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> are, respectively, responsible for the reflection signals at 26.7°, 31.4°, 38.1°, 50.2°, and 55.1°. While the additional peak signals appearing at 30.2°, 34.7°, 46.1°, and 53.5°, assignable to the (1 0 0), (1 0 1), (1 0 2), and (1 1 0) facets, originate from the NiS, symbolizing the successful construction of the bimetallic sulfides heterostructure. Two noticeable shifts in the Raman spectrum of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2B</a>, are visible at ≈1 386.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> (disordered graphitic carbon) and 1 585.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> (sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized graphitic carbon), which, depending on the carbon substance, is categorized as the <i>D</i>-band and the <i>G</i>-band, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0039_R_d83639732e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, estimating the unordered degree of carbonaceous substance involved computing the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> proportion, thus, the computed value of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> for the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites is ∼0.99, suggesting that surface carbon atom structure is influenced by sufficiently high conductivity and more polar C–N/C–S species. Besides, the existence of NiS is a main reason for three relatively weak characteristic signals that emerged in a low wavenumber in the Raman spectrogram of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites. In particular, the peaks at around 506.3 and 673.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> might be connected to the Ni–S bond mode,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0040_R_d83639732e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem Eng J">40</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0041_R_d83639732e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem Eng J">41</a></sup></span> whereas the peak at 428.9 cm<sup>−1</sup> is interpreted as the stretching mode of the S–S pair.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0042_R_d83639732e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Appl Surf Sci">42</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0043_R_d83639732e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J Alloy Compd">43</a></sup></span> The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption experiments of four samples and the matching pore size distribution are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2C,D</a>, one can see that the representative IV adsorption/desorption isotherms with clear hysteresis loop that appear at the <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> of 0.70–0.95 suggest the mesoporous structure existed in these samples. On the basis of the measurement results, Brunauer–Emmett–Teller surface area of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, bare Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and NiS were determined to be about 29.4, 8.86, 25.3, and 10.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, with the majority of pore diameters at 18.4, 30.6, 19.4, and 26.9&nbsp;nm, respectively. Such superior mesoporous structure and comparatively large surface area of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites offer essential advantages for the complete penetration of the electrolyte and fast Na<sup>+</sup>-transport and charge transference rate, guaranteeing a greater contact area between the electrode and electrolyte, and thereby efficiently enhancing the electrochemical activity. The carbon content in Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites was verified using thermal gravimetric (TG) technique (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2E</a>). Obviously, surface water desorption is responsible for a small weight loss (2.68 wt%) of the sample below 200°C. Following that, a significant drop in weight occurs in a range of 350°C–550°C, which could potentially be attributable to the combination of carbon with oxygen to form carbon dioxide. The main weight loss at 650°C–800°C is due to the formation of NiO and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> during the oxidation reaction,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0044_R_d83639732e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem Eng J">44</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0045_R_d83639732e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Electrochim Acta">45</a></sup></span> the calculated amount of the carbon matrix was estimated to be ∼34.9%. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was proceeded to ascertain the bonding configuration and electronic coupling effect in more detail between Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS. As depicted in full survey spectra of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2F</a>, the reflection signals of all the elements in four samples can be found. Note that the as-prepared sample will inevitably be exposed to air, which result in the superficial oxidation of the metal sulfides to produce amorphous metal oxides. More importantly, XPS is a surface analysis technique, and the analysis depth of conventional tests is usually around 10&nbsp;nm, thus inducing the presence of a significant O 1s peak in the survey XPS spectrum. Two peaks, Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at 781.8&nbsp;eV and Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 797.9&nbsp;eV, are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2G</a> for high-resolution Co 2p spectra, while the other peaks associated with Co<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are linked to binding energies of 779.4 and 794.4&nbsp;eV, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0018_R_d83639732e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></sup></span> Similarly, two valence states related to Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup> in the high-resolution Ni 2p spectra are discernible (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2H</a>), where two doublets at 854.1 and 871.5&nbsp;eV are, respectively, linked to Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals, while another group of distinctive peaks at 856.8 and 874.8&nbsp;eV connected with Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0044_R_d83639732e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem Eng J">44</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0046_R_d83639732e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem Eng J">46</a></sup></span> Regarding the S 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0002">2I</a>), four fitted peaks at 161.5, 162.4, 163.8, and 168.3&nbsp;eV are, respectively, attributable to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of divalent sulfide ions, thiophene-S, and SO<sub>x</sub> species.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0035_R_d83639732e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Mater Chem A">35</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0047_R_d83639732e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv Energy Mater">47</a></sup></span> Notably, a special peak signal centered at 164.8&nbsp;eV position of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C can be correspond to C–S/C = S bonds.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0039_R_d83639732e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0048_R_d83639732e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Small Struct">48</a></sup></span> The peaks at 284.8, 285.2, 286.4, and 288.3&nbsp;eV, which are associated with the C–C, C = C, C–S/C–N, and C = O, respectively,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0049_R_d83639732e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - SusMat">49</a>-<a href="#sus2198-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0051_R_d83639732e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Electrochim Acta">51</a></sup></span> can be fitted to the high-resolution C 1s spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>. The successful doping of the carbon matrix by N and S atoms can generate plentiful active sites to accommodate more Na<sup>+</sup>, and distinctly accelerate the electronic transfer efficiency. As for N 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>), three peaks are visible, representing pyridinic-N (398.5&nbsp;eV), pyrrolic-N (400.2&nbsp;eV), and quaternary-N (401.1&nbsp;eV).<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0052_R_d83639732e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sus2198-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e624cd1-2a93-4b68-b45c-a7c5353d348c/sus2198-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e624cd1-2a93-4b68-b45c-a7c5353d348c/sus2198-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1b1f93f9-70cd-4da9-a87b-ea43fd88ba5f/sus2198-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e624cd1-2a93-4b68-b45c-a7c5353d348c/sus2198-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">FIGURE 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sus2198-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsus2.198" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) XRD spectra, (C) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm, and (D) pore size distribution curves of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C together with the three reference samples. (B) Raman spectra of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C and Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS. (E) TGA profile of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. XPS spectra of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C together with three reference samples: (F) XPS survey spectra, high-resolution (G) Co 2p, (H) Ni 2p, (I) S 2p spectra. XRD, X-ray diffraction; TGA, thermal gravimetric analysis; XPS, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles for first three cycles of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites in conjunction with the reference samples, that is, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>), Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>), and NiS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a>) were tested at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to collect their redox mechanism during the Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion. As for Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3A</a>), the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> crystal structure to stimulate the formation of Na<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> is a dominant reason that causes the first-cycle peak to occur at 1.25&nbsp;V in the first cathodic scan thanks to the conversion of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0053_R_d83639732e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nanoscale">53</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0054_R_d83639732e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J Mater Chem A">54</a></sup></span> and then this peak shift to 1.40&nbsp;V in the following cycles. Besides, the reduction of NiS to Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub> that is forming on the initial Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion stage is what induces a sharp peak emerged at 1.13&nbsp;V.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0055_R_d83639732e1166" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Chem Eng J">55</a></sup></span> While the successive conversion of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub> to Ni, Co, and Na<sub>2</sub>S accompanied with the formation of the SEI layer well affords persuasive explanation for the observed broad reduction peak spanning from 0.50 to 0.90&nbsp;V.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0018_R_d83639732e1183" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></sup></span> In the first anodic scan, the corresponding oxidation peaks occurred at 1.76, 1.95, and 2.09&nbsp;V are attributable to the desalting reaction to form Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS. The well-preserved CV curves in subsequent cycles indicate that the sodiation/desodiation reaction of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode exhibits strong reversibility following the initial activation cycle. Ex situ XPS analysis was applied to ascertain the elemental valence for the cycled electrodes during the oxidation and reduction. The high-resolution Co 2p spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3B</a> reveal that when the electrode was discharged from 1.0&nbsp;V to a fully stated 0.01&nbsp;V, the Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peak clearly moves to a low binding energy, simultaneously the Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p peak areas gradually decreases, signifying the partial oxidation of Co (II) to Co (III). More significantly, a noticeable distinctive peak signal associated with Co<sup>0</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> appears,<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0056_R_d83639732e1209" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Electrochim Acta">56</a></sup></span> indexing to the formation of metallic Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S from the conversion reaction of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>. When the charge reaches its maximum, Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p recovers to its original state proving well reversibility. A similar changing trend is seen in the high-resolution Ni 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3C</a>). The distinctive Ni<sup>0</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> species peak at around 856.3&nbsp;eV is identified after the battery was fully discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, followed by completely vanish as the voltage changed to 3.0&nbsp;V.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0057_R_d83639732e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nanoscale">57</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0058_R_d83639732e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nanoscale">58</a></sup></span> A series of distinctive signal peaks that are connected to polythionate and thiosulfate were seen from the high-resolution S 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3D</a>), which are found in the bonding-energy region of 166 ∼172&nbsp;eV.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0016_R_d83639732e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem Eng J">16</a></sup></span> In addition, the emergence of polar S<sup>2</sup><sub>2-</sub> and S<sup>2</sup><i><sub>n</sub></i><sub>-</sub> bond at 162–164.5&nbsp;eV indicates the high chemical adsorption capabilities at the fully charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0059_R_d83639732e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J Mater Chem A">59</a></sup></span> Further, a comprehensive understanding of voltage-dependent fundamental phase change and Na-storage mechanism of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C upon the first sodiation/desodiation was obtained through the combination of a suite of ex situ characterizations, such as XRD, HRTEM, and SAED patterns. It is significant to bear in mind that, to increase the peak signal intensity of intermediate product, ex situ XRD signals of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C were gathered by applying aluminum foil as current collector. Meanwhile, coloration that ranges from light purple to red denote a peak intensity characterized by strengthening. Prior to the discharging, as-fabricated Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3E,F</a>, is clearly capable of detecting the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> peaks at 26.7°, 31.4°, 38.1°, 50.2°, and 55.0° together with NiS peaks at 30.2°, 34.7°, 46.1°, and 53.5°, these signals exhibit a slight migration toward a lower 2θ owing to the crystal lattice expansion resulting from the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, followed by the gradual disappearance. Meanwhile, a new reflection signal emerged at about 16.0°, attributable to the (1 1 0) of intermediate Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS #79-2145), represents the occurrence of the intercalation reaction when the electrode was discharged to 1.0&nbsp;V. There is the noticeable color increase at 19.1°, 31.6°, and 34.7° that are indexed to the (2 2 2), (4 0 4), and (6 1 1) facets of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS #47-0178) and is caused by the conversion reaction from the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>NiS phase to Fe/Ni and Na<sub>2</sub>S phases as the potential drops to 0.01&nbsp;V. Following the charging process, the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> reflections with the (1 3 0) facet at 27.6° and (2 2 0) facet at 32.3° emerge in tandem with the gradual weakening and final vanishing of the Na<sub>2</sub>S phase with the charging process continues to 1.6&nbsp;V, further revealing the reversible transformation process of Fe/Ni and Na<sub>2</sub>S to Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>NiS phases. Subsequently, the de-intercalation reaction happened as the electrode was further charged from 1.5 to 3.0&nbsp;V, resulting in the re-formation of the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> phase corresponding to the (4 0 0) plane at 38.1°, demonstrating good reversibility of electrochemical reaction for Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>. Notably, the lack of distinct peaks originating from the reformed Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>NiS, Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, and NiS at this stage was discovered, which is linked to either their poor crystallinity or the complete covering of the amorphous SEI film on the electrode surface. All these discoveries line up with our prediction of multistep phase-evolution reactions with the intermediate phase generation, and are also compatible with the CV results. Besides, ex situ HRTEM graphs and SAED patterns at four specific voltage points provide additional evidence for these conclusions. Distinctly, the byproduct of the intercalation reaction, that is, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, is clearly visible in the HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3G</a>) and corresponding SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3H</a>) after the battery is depleted to roughly 1.0&nbsp;V, suggesting the good consistency with above ex situ XRD results. Further the voltage decreases to 0.01&nbsp;V resulted in the labeling of the (1 1 1) plane of Ni/Co together with the (6 1 1) and (2 4 6) planes of Na<sub>2</sub>S at the interplanar spacings of 0.198, 0.216, and 0.256&nbsp;nm, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3I</a>). Meanwhile, the distinctive diffraction rings of by-product Na<sub>2</sub>S in the corresponding SAED pattern provide overwhelming support for this (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3J</a>), standing out the occurrence of the conversion reaction. Once the electrode was recharged to 1.6&nbsp;V, the (1 1 2), (2 4 0), and (2 0 2) planes of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> are represented by the three nanocrystal regions in the HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3K</a>) with distinct crystal lattices of 0.256, 0.210, and 0.215&nbsp;nm, respectively. Furthermore, the SAED diffraction spots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3L</a>) corroborate the presence of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanocrystals, which supports the ex situ HRTEM result. The HRTEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3M</a> validates the lattice distances of 0.238 and 0.297&nbsp;nm at a fully charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V, which can be correlated to the (4 0 0) plane of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and the (1 0 0) plane of NiS. Correspondingly, three diffraction rings that are indexed to the (2 2 0) and (4 2 2) planes of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and (2 2 0) facet of NiS and in the SAED image also manifest the good reversibility of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C composites toward the electrochemical reaction as well as its structural integrity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3N</a>). A detailed graphical description of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode can be illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0003">3O</a> based on the above ex situ detection.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sus2198-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a09b67b-2a71-4562-8951-f73d05fb4918/sus2198-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a09b67b-2a71-4562-8951-f73d05fb4918/sus2198-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e905c3f0-9252-4b4d-8b90-c552f02f6895/sus2198-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a09b67b-2a71-4562-8951-f73d05fb4918/sus2198-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">FIGURE 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sus2198-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsus2.198" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) CV curves at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. Ex situ high-resolution XPS spectra at different initial discharge–charge states: (B) Co 2p, (C) Ni 2p, and (D) S 2p. (E,F) Ex situ XRD patterns at different initial discharge–charge states. Ex situ HRTEM and SAED patterns at discharged states of (G,H) 1.0&nbsp;V, (I,J) 0.01&nbsp;V, and charged states of (K,L) 1.60&nbsp;V, (M,N) 3.0&nbsp;V. (O) The graphical illustration of phase-transformation of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. CV, Cyclic voltammetry; XPS, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy; XRD, X-ray diffraction; SAED, selected area electron diffraction.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To assess the effect of the outer-carbon encapsulation and heterogeneous interface on the electrochemical features of TMCs, the voltage-capacity curves of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C and reference samples, that is, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and NiS, are also recorded at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In particular, the initial galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles for the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4A</a>) present two voltage platforms, which match Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction and are also in good agreement with the CV results. Meanwhile, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode provides the remarkable capacities of 903.5 and 829.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the first discharge and charge processes accompanying an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of up to 91.8 %, presenting the remarkable capacity enhancement in contrast with those of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS (754.8/731.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>), Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (602.8/662.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>), and NiS (560.7/579.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a>), further suggesting the improved reaction reversibility and high capacity contribution of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. Meanwhile, a relatively lower ICE value of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C than other three samples maybe due to the introduction of non-treated carbon source with poor sodium storage ability. Afterward, a remarkable reversible capacity of 705.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is maintained after 60 cycles with a capacity retention of 78.0% when the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode was galvanically cycled at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4B</a>). In contrast, the reversible capacities for Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and NiS are only 624.4, 537.4, and 508.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The superiority of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C is further emphasized by its superior rate capability. In especial, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode at 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> was found to have highly reversible discharge capacities of 747.4, 688.9, 643.2, 600.6, 549.0, and 497.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4C</a>). Surprised, we can observe that even when the electrode cycled at spectacularly 20.0 and 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the significantly reversible discharge capacities reaching up to 420.7 and 354.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be respectively maintained, which can almost reset to the original 755.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the current returns to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the extraordinary rate capability and wonderful structural stability. More interestingly, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode has a significantly better rate capability, as evidenced by its 1.2, 1.5, and 1.8 times higher reversible capacity at 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> in comparison to the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and NiS electrodes. Meanwhile, a comprehensive comparison of specific capacity against the current rate between Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C and state-of-art Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> or NiS-based anodes for SIBs was conducted, we can see that the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode stands out on the electrochemical performance among those of SIB anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4D</a>)<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0018_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0021_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J Ind Eng Chem">21</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0054_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J Mater Chem A">54</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0055_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Chem Eng J">55</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0060_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Ceram Int">60</a>-<a href="#sus2198-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0069_R_d83639732e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Small">69</a></sup></span> establishing that the self-constructed electric field stemmed from the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS heterogeneous interface together with the outer-carbon encapsulation on the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS nanosphere surface considerably enhance the rate performance reversibility and electrochemical reaction kinetics. Moreover, by graphing the connection between average charge voltage and current rate at a charge potential platform of ∼1.52&nbsp;V through the GCD curves of four electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>) at different rates, a comparison of structural pulverization for four electrodes at different rates was conducted. Notably, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode manifests the lowest polarizability in above-mentioned electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4E</a>) thanks to a smallest voltage change of only 0.30&nbsp;V with the current rate increasing from 0.2 to 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (0.35&nbsp;V for Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS, 0.44&nbsp;V for Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and 0.62&nbsp;V for NiS), standing out its highest electrical conductivity and fastest electron transport. The resistance difference between the electrode and electrolyte was further validated by the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) to further account for the reason of increased kinetics behavior of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. The resulting equivalent circuit is inferred and depicted in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4F</a>, obviously, a semicircle and an intercept at the real axis denote charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and solvent/ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) in the high-frequency zone, respectively, while Warburg impedance (W) is discernible as a sloping line in the low-frequency range. Through the mentioned equivalent circuit, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode manifests the smallest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and the slope value of <i>Z</i>'∼ ω<sup>1/2</sup> plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4G</a>) in four electrodes, meaning its fastest charge-transfer rate and Na<sup>+</sup>-ion reaction kinetics, as well as highest electronic conductivity in view of the synergistic effect of the constructed Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS heterostructure and outer-carbon encapsulation. The electrochemical reaction kinetics of as-prepared electrodes is in situ monitored at various discharge–charge phases through exploring the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), and a schematic diagram of the GITT calculation and the detailed GITT description is illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>. Observed from GITT profiles of four electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4H</a>), the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode affords a smaller overpotential and more remarkable ion-diffusion kinetics in the range of 10<sup>−10</sup> to10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> during the whole first discharge–charge cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4I</a>), significantly superior to those of other three electrodes, foreboding faster Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion kinetics in Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. The outstanding kinetics feature can be believed to be caused by the stimulation effect on the electron/ion transportation that results from synergistically induced Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS interfacial interaction and better conductivity of the outer-carbon layer. The ultralong cyclic lifespan of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C was operated under an extremely high current rate of 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It is observed in inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0004">4J</a> that a discharge–charge process takes only 160.0 s to complete according to the sectional GCD curves of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C electrode at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and meanwhile, the almost identical outline of the profiles also signifies excellent structural durability and stable electrochemical phase-evolution of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C. Particularly as evidence of exceptional cycling stability, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/NiS@N-C maintains an appealing discharge capacity reaching 310.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an extremely small capacity fading of only 0.05% each cycle even after 2 400 cycles, which significantly outperforms the previous Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> or NiS-based anodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), verifying its extraordinary cycling stability and superior reversibility.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="sus2198-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6d419f61-3ab8-4f1f-9d02-1ed9a26cc52e/sus2198-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6d419f61-3ab8-4f1f-9d02-1ed9a26cc52e/sus2198-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f37d3b18-378e-4516-b083-749dc74144bb/sus2198-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6d419f61-3ab8-4f1f-9d02-1ed9a26cc52e/sus2198-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">FIGURE 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sus2198-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsus2.198" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To systematically shed light on the inherent difference and origin in high-rate capabilities of four electrodes, the electrochemical reaction kinetics characteristics in the discharge–charge processes were thoroughly examined by separating the charge-store mechanisms induced by the surface-capacitive and diffusion behaviors with the use of CV at various scan rates, varying from 0.2 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. One can observe that the CV curves of each electrode show nearly the same profile outlines and variation tendency at different scan rates, and meanwhile their redox peaks are well conserved even at a high rate of 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sus2198-fig-0005">5A</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a,d,g</a>), revealing the rapid and reversible electrochemical process without a noticeable phase transition.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0019_R_d83639732e1800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem Eng J">19</a></sup></span> Simultaneously, it is readily apparent that, as the scan speed increases, the anodic peaks move toward a higher potential, whereas the cathodic peaks underwent a decrease in potential. The sustainable shift of redox peaks in the opposite direction illustrates the weak polarization of the electrode in the electrolyte and the presence of combined diffusion-controlled and surface capacitive behaviors.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0070_R_d83639732e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Small">70</a></sup></span> The connection between peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) can be used to determine whether the current is regulated by an ion-diffusion reaction or a pseudocapacitive-controlled reaction.<span><sup><a href="#sus2198-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0005_R_d83639732e1811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv Mater">5</a>, <a href="#sus2198-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sus2198-bib-0012_R_d83639732e1811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J Mater Chem A">12</a></sup></span>
